                        <span style="padding-left: 5px;">
                            <strong style="font-size:15pt;">David Price</strong></span>
                        <div class="spacer_10"></div>
                        <div style="font-size: 10pt; padding-left: 5px;">

                <tr id="so1" style="background-color: #FFF;"><td colspan="5" style="border-top:2px solid #D3D3D3; padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="width:100%; font-size:10pt"><center>Profiles:&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa2" href="javascript:showMoreSO();">2010</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa3" href="javascript:showMoreSO2011();">2011</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa4" href="javascript:showMoreSO2012();">2012</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa5" href="javascript:showMoreSO2013();">2013</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa6" href="javascript:showMoreSO2014();">2014</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa7" href="javascript:showMoreSO2015();">2015</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa8" href="javascript:showMoreSO2016();">2016</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;<a id="soa9"  href="javascript:showMoreSO2017();">2017</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;(Click Year to Expand / <a href="javascript:hideSO('so1');">Close</a>)</center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>Perhaps baseball’s top pitching prospect heading into the 2009 season, much was made of Price starting the year in Triple-A. After a Scott Kazmir DL stint, though, Price was right up and showed he could use some extra seasoning. The Rays kept him in the majors despite a few rocky starts and, while a 4.59 FIP doesn’t match the hype, it’s still a step in the right direction for someone making their debut in the American League East. Price’s HR/FB ratio is a bit on the high end and could sink down next year, which would help his run average metrics.<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>Midway through the season Price began throwing more two-seam fastballs, which may have driven his impressive second half thanks to an increase in ground balls. After June/July his GB% went up to 44.9 and 44.1% in the remaining months.  There’s no doubt he’ll start 2010 in the Rays rotation, likely as the team’s third starter. He still needs to develop confidence in his secondary stuff, including a change-up and spike curve that have the makings of – at worst – league-average offerings. Late in the year, Price was throwing fewer sliders, too, but more change-ups; it will be interesting to see if that trend continues into 2010. Price should earn close to double-digit victories while posting a below-league-average ERA. (R.J. Anderson)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>This is what incremental -- and therefore hopefully sustainable -- improvement in a young pitcher looks like. By changing his pitching mix just a little, Price added a strikeout per nine, subtracted half a walk per nine, and upped his ground-ball rate two percent (to 43.7%). He still uses the fastball a lot (74%), but linear weights didn't mind (+23.5 runs above average). In fact, his fastball was ninth-best in the league by that statistic. There was some luck involved in his season, as his BABIP (.279) and strand rate (78.5%) were both better than the means in those categories. If his luck returns to normal, though, there's still a chance that similar marginal improvement will mask most of the regression. Sure, he won't have an ERA in the mid-twos -- that's tough in the American League -- but he could easily manage an ERA in the mid-threes, with a bushel of wins. (Eno Sarris)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>The Rays' fire-balling lefty brings one of the best fastballs in the league and is already a top starter. Further work on his pitching mix could lead to more true-talent improvement, even if that step forward will likely be masked by regression in some luck statistics.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Some look at Price’s 2011 season as a disappointment. He had a losing record and his ERA of 3.49 was nearly a full run higher than 2010. The thing is, he pitched better. His strikeout rate increased to 8.75 per nine while his walks fell to 2.53 per nine. His 3.46 strikeout-to-walk ratio ranked eighth in the AL. He encountered some bad luck with men in scoring position, allowing a .331 batting average on balls in play in those situations. He pitched well enough to win more than 12 games, but the Rays offense had trouble scoring for him, as evidenced by their 3.93 runs per nine in his starts. Given better luck, the type he had in 2010, there’s no reason Price shouldn’t return to fantasy prominence. (Erik Hahmann)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Price is still one of the best pitchers in the game. Advanced metrics point toward a lower ERA, and improved luck, both of the offense and batted ball variety, should help his win total improve.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>When talking about the reigning AL Cy Young winner, praise comes pretty easy. You know what you’re getting. He’s going to strike out 200+, he’s going to throw 200+ innings, he’s going to have a low ERA, and he’s not going to walk many people. The amount of wins he gets can fluctuate so predicting that is often futile, see Cliff Lee last season. This will be Price’s fourth full season, and he’s gradually gotten better in every one of them. Last season, while leading the American League in ERA, he threw his fastball just 60% of the time, implementing a cutter that helped his ground-ball rate shoot up to 53%. He’s an ace in every sense of the word and will rightly be among the top five pitchers taken in most drafts. (Erik Hahmann)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Along with Justin Verlander, the 27-year-old Price is the model of elite consistency, putting up 200 innings and strikeouts while maintaining a low ERA every season.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Despite missing six weeks with a scary sounding triceps injury, Price enjoyed another strong year, though he took a slightly different path this time around. His strikeout rate fell to its lowest mark since his rookie 2009 campaign, but he displayed the best control of his career to make up for those missing punchouts. But relying on pinpoint control rather than swing and miss stuff is riskier and his fastball velocity dropped two miles per hour from 2012. Price has been the subject of heavy trade rumors and given the various red flags in his profile, it would stand to reason that the Rays know exactly what they are doing. If he is indeed sent packing, his new team will obviously play a role in his fantasy value, but either way, he might be someone to shy away from assuming you would have to pay ace prices to secure him on your roster. (<a href="https://twitter.com/MikePodhorzer" target="_blank">Mike Podhorzer)</a><br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Over the past four seasons, Price has been one of the best pitchers in baseball. But an ominous sounding triceps injury during the season, plus a decrease in velocity, strikeout rate and swinging strike rate, suggest that fantasy owners should do as the Rays are doing -- stay away from him in 2014 unless he comes at a discount.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Although he posted an ERA above his career average, Price actually finished fourth in the American League in SIERA. His strikeout minus walk rate was third best in baseball, as he maintained the suddenly pinpoint control he displayed in 2013, while also boosting his strikeout rate to a career high level. Only a career worst .306 batting average on balls in play pushed his ERA above three. Both his two-seam and four-seam fastballs generated swinging strikes at significantly higher clips than he previously averaged, despite not gaining any velocity. It's the primary explanation behind that strikeout rate surge, but seems a bit unsustainable. He did use his excellent changeup more often, however, which could allow him to keep some of his gains if he uses the pitch with the same frequency again. Unfortunately, the trade deadline blockbuster that sent him from the Rays to the Tigers robs him of the benefits he received by pitching half his games in the more pitcher-friendly Tropicana Field. Furthermore, his defensive support in Detroit is unlikely to be as good as it had been.  Price should remain an elite hurler, but he's ever so slightly less attractive in Detroit than he was in Tampa Bay. (<a href="https://www.projectingx.com" target="_blank" onclick="return false;">Mike Podhorzer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>A spike in strikeout rate paired with elite control resulted in a dominating season, even if his ERA doesn't tell that story exactly. A full season in Detroit is a bit less desirable than one in Tampa Bay, but he should remain a top tier fantasy starter.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>For the second year in a row, Price was dealt mid-season to help a contender reach the playoffs. The results were even better the second time around, as Price posted a 2.30 ERA for the Blue Jays down the stretch, giving him a career best 2.45 mark by year's end. His 2.78 FIP also matched a career best, and there's little indication that he's slowing down at age 30. Now the recipient of a seven year, $217 million contract, the move to Boston shouldn't concern owners -- Price has pitched 43.7% of his career innings against AL East opponents with a 3.17 ERA, and he's been ridiculous at Fenway. As he's matured, Price has leaned more heavily on his changeup, and he's gotten more horizontal movement and swinging strikes with it year-over-year, helping him neutralize the platoon advantage two years running (jamming righties inside with his cutter has helped, too). Getting a mile an hour back on his fastball is an encouraging sign that no decline is forthcoming, and there's little to suggest he won't stick around the 25% strikeout rate and 5:1 strikeout to walk ratio he generally enjoys. He's a sure-fire top 10 arm, perhaps even top five for the coming year. (Blake Murphy)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Another new home, same old David Price. The terrific fastball / changeup combo helped lead him to perhaps his best statistical season yet, and there's nothing to suggest he shouldn't be one of the top pitchers off the board again.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
                <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Price was one of two pitchers who signed for an average annual value north of $30 million dollars in the 2016 offseason and like Zack Greinke, he underperformed against expectations. Like Greinke, Price’s biggest issue was a surge in home runs that added over a run and a half to his earned run average. Greinke had an oblique injury and some degradation in his skills to help explain the backslide, but Price’s skills were nearly identical to his 2015 and he made a career-high 35 starts. We learned of a mechanical issue for Price that was spotted by Dustin Pedroia and it looked like the solution to the problems. Price reeled off an eight-start run of 2.47 ERA with 56 strikeouts in 58.3 innings immediately after fixing the mechanics, but he couldn’t maintain the ace level rest of the season. He allowed 10+ hits in five of his final 20 starts leading to a solid, but decidedly non-ace-like 3.80 ERA/1.27 WHIP combo down the stretch. The good news is that his base skills and health even in the face of the home run barrage. The bad news is that his new home park played a role in those homers (1.3 HR/9 at Fenway). The worst news is that he missed time in Spring Training with a balky elbow which casts a shadow over his 2017 outlook. The perceived depth of frontline arms eroded in 2016 and despite his issues, Price remains among that group, though it's now on the backend given the elbow concerns. (<a href="http://twitter.com/sporer">Paul Sporer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>In his Boston debut, Price had an ERA a half run higher than anything we’d ever seen from him since he joined the ace ranks and a run and a half higher than his 2015 total, yet his strikeout rate dropped a single percentage point and his walk rate was flat. Outside of a bevy of homers – which were likely related to some mechanical issues that he fixed for periods throughout the season – he was the ace we’ve grown used to seeing. Don’t overrate 2016 and drop Price too far, he’s still a stud, though his calling card durability is now in question thanks to some elbow trouble in the spring.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
            </table>
